### Exercise 3: Check AP Style in a Story

I've edited several AP Stylebook errors into the three paragraphs of copy below. You can use [Claude.ai](http://Claude.ai) or ChatGPT to fix the AP Style by using this prompt. You also can try it out with your own stories.

**Prompt and Copy.** Edit the following text for AP Style and spelling:

> Five Cook County employees defrauded the federal COVID-19 relief program of more than two-hundred and forty thousand dollars, falsely claiming they owned companies that struggled during the pandemic, the county inspector general's office said in a report Friday.
>
> Over the past year, twenty other county workers also were suspected of defrauding the federal Paycheck Protection Program, accoring to interum Inspector General Steven Cyranoski. They include three high-ranking county officials, including one in the county's human resources office, a payroll supervisor in the comptroller's office & a director in the health department, according to Cyranoski.
>
> In five quarterly reports since July 2022, Cyranoski said he & his investigators found county workers were suspected of stealing more than seven hundred thousand dollars in PPP money.

\* \* \*

::: section
### Exercise 4: Let's Search for Some Public Records

Use Candid, the ProPublica Nonprofit Explorer or Charity Watch to search for Form 990 documents on nonprofits revenues. Let's start with the National Rifle Association and answer these questions:

1.   In the most recent year, what were the NRA's revenues and expenditures?
2.  How much did the NRA's CEO make?
3.  Look back through Form 990s from years past and you'll see some familiar names: Oliver North and rock star Ted Nugent. How much did they earn serving on the board? How many hours did they put in for the week?

- **Candid 990 Finder:** [https://candid.org/research-and-verify-nonprofits/990-finder](https://candid.org)
- **ProPublica Nonprofit Explorer:** [https://projects.propublica.org/nonprofits](https://projects.propublica.org)
- **CharityWatch:** Rankings and lists of highest-paid executives. [www.charitywatch.org](http://www.charitywatch.org)

If you need help, this training video walks you through how to use these tools using some different examples: <https://bit.ly/charity990> (Start at the 11-minute mark)

\* \* \*

::: section
### Exercise 5: Violation Tracker

The Violation Tracker is the first wide-ranging database on corporate misconduct. Produced by the Corporate Research Project of Good Jobs First, it covers banking, consumer protection, false claims, environmental, wage and hour, safety, discrimination, price-fixing and other cases resolved by federal regulatory agencies and parts of the Justice Department.

For this exercise:

1.  Go to the Violation Tracker ([www.goodjobsfirst.org/violation-tracker](http://www.goodjobsfirst.org)) and click on Advanced Search in the far lower-right corner of the home page.
2.  This will give you a page full of drop-down menus. Use the pulldown menu labeled State/Local Agency on the right side and select the Environmental Protection Agency for your state.
3.  Click down through the results to find specific fines and penalties for companies in your state. Which company has been fined the most times? The most money? You can click down through the text links and find details on the fines, settlements and lawsuits. How could you turn this into a story?.

You can total the fines in your state or city over a period of years and compare them to other cities and states.

If you need help, this training video walks you through how to use Violation Tracker: <https://bit.ly/charity990>

\* \* \*

::: section
### Exercise 6: Train ChatGPT to Understand Your Reporting and Editing Needs

Use these prompts to improve your writing and reporting. The results may not be perfect -- just as a web search as imperfect -- so be selective in what results you use.

- **Writing style:**
- Analyze the text below for style, voice and tone. Create a prompt to write a new paragraph in the same style, voice and tone:

<!-- -->

- **Editing your work:**
- Edit the following text. It's for my \[newsletter/paragraph/essay\]. \[paste text\]
- **Memorization:**
- What are the most important facts, dates or formulas related to \[topic\]? Help me create a memorization technique to remember them easily.
- **Learn from a mistake:**
- I made a mistake while practicing \[skill\]. Can you explain what went wrong and how I can avoid making the same mistake in the future?
- **Simplify complex information:**
- Break down the \[topic\] into smaller, easier-to-understand parts. Use analogies and real-life examples to simplify the concept and make it more relatable.
- **Funny way to simplify:**
- Rewrite the \[text\] as if I was a 10-year-old.
- **Summarize into bullet points:**
- Summarize this article into a bulleted list of the most important information \[paste article\].
- **Change writing style:**
- Change the writing style of the text below to \[tone\] \[paste text\] Use case: Easily make an informative paragraph humorous and easier to read.
- **Brainstorming:**
- Brainstorm 20 trending ideas for a Twitter thread on recent breaking AI news (or another topic)

# 2 Searching the Web and Using AI in Research

DOI: [10.4324/9781003431787-3](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003431787-3){aria-label="D.O.I. link to this document."}


**Key Resource Pages**

Beyond the basic search tools, here are some key resources we'll explore in this chapter:

- EUR-lex: [https://eur-lex.europa.eu/collection/eu-law/eu-case-law.html](https://eur-lex.europa.eu)
- European Union case law
- Google Advanced Search Operators: [https://docs.google.com/document/d/1ydVaJJeL1EYbWtlfj9TPfBTE5IBADkQfZrQaBZxqXGs/edit](https://docs.google.com)
- Google Trends: [https://trends.google.com/home](https://trends.google.com)
- Journalist's Toolbox AI Search Tools: [http://journaliststoolbox.ai/ai-driven-search](http://journaliststoolbox.ai)
- [Newspapers.com](https://www.Newspapers.com): [www.newspapers.com](http://www.newspapers.com)
- Public Access to Court Electronic Records: [https://pacer.uscourts.gov/PACER](https://pacer.uscourts.gov)
- US government's portal for federal court records. Federal court is different from city, state, appellate and other court systems.
- The Wayback Machine: <https://archive.org>

\* \* \*

**Editor's note:** With the influx of AI-driven search tools being developed in 2023, some of the tools covered in this chapter may appear different from how they do in the book. Also, reverse image search tools can be found in [Chapter 3](#ch03.xhtml__15031-5909_ch003)'s fact-checking exercises, and ChatGPT and Google Bard search exercises are in [Chapter 11](#ch11.xhtml__15031-5909_ch011).

\* \* \*

CEO Sundar Pichai stepped on the stage at the Google I/O 2023 conference in May and everything we knew about Google search started to change. One by one, Pichai introduced heads of various departments from the tech giant, which had been struggling to keep up with AI tools from other companies.

But May 10, 2023, changed all that. Google moved several new search features and add-ons to its current products that present many new options to its long-reliable search algorithm.

Unlike Google's stark-white results page with 10 blue links, its generative AI results appear in colorful boxes above the normal search results. Google scrapes information from all over the web and presents it in a simple list, putting ChatGPT-style generative AI results front and center in the results page.

The changes weren't just to Google search. The company also announced:

- Maps with Immersive View for Routes, Magic Editor for Photos, and interactive ChatGPT-type dialogue in Google Search
- Google Bard, the company's answer to ChatGPT, integrated with Adobe Firefly to allow text-to-image capabilities in Bard.
- Google Sheets, which offers a "Help Me Organize" feature on the right side of the sheet that helps you build tables right into a template. For instance, you can author a prompt telling it to build a table for tracking automobile thefts in Munich, Germany, and you'll get a table header built with columns in which to flow data. As with an AI-generated template, you'll want to edit it to your specific needs. This can be helpful not just for data reporters but for invoicing, budgeting, etc., for freelancers.


<em>Figure</em> <em>2.1</em> Google generative AI results. Note the prompts at the bottom that offer more detailed search


While AI offers new opportunities for journalistic research, it also opens a Pandora's box of potential issues. This chapter focuses on how to search the Internet to find valid information, sources and data for news stories. While much of it explores new AI features in Google, Bing and other tools, it also offers guidance on searching the deep web, how to track searches, how to search social media and how to hack a website address to find archived information in databases.

:::: section
## Changes in the Works with Google Search

Google slowly rolled out its "AI overhaul" through its Search Labs site over a period of weeks in the summer of 2023. With some searches, Google will show a snapshot of an AI-driven chatbot response at the top of the results, suggest prompts for follow-up questions and provide more links at the top with the typical search results underneath. Clicking on the prompts opens a conversational module right in the search page. This helps with searches that don't always have a clear answer, so AI can help tailor the search more.

Borrowing a page from TikTok, Google added a "Perspectives" filter at the top of the search page displaying user-generated content such as long- and short-form videos, posts from social media sites and discussion boards. Information about the creators and the popularity of the post appear at the top, which is helpful for fact-checking.

\* \* \*


<em>Figure</em> <em>2.2</em> Traditional Google search result, without generative AI


<aside aria-label="box1" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">


::: section
## Search Tools 

Tips and tricks for using Google search operators and other search shortcuts.

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=vcprR-KL3vo](http://www.youtube.com)

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## Google Search Operators

Even with generative AI, journalists still use search operators to expedite their search queries.

Samantha Sunne, a freelance investigative data journalist and co-author of the textbook "Data + Journalism," uses Google search operators to search more efficiently. She often uses filters such as "site:" to better perform searches for specific phrases or specific files buried deep within a specific site.

"The general rule, when searching for a person, a story, or just about anything, is to start specific and work your way out from there," Sunne said. "There are many times when I think, 'There is no way this website is going to have exactly what I'm looking for.' But I type in the exact term, usually using quotes to denote an exact phrase and see what comes up.

"Every once in a while, it will work. More often, I will need to cut out a word or search for a more umbrella term, and then skim or read whatever comes up."

Search operators help expedite that process. Dan Russell, formerly a senior research scientist at Google, has compiled a list of dozens of search operators to help you find the information you want.

**Filetype:** This operator helps you search the web for a specific type of file:. doc,. xlsx,. ppt,. pdf. Type this into a Google search field and see the results:

Filetype:xlsx Sydney Australia positive COVID-19 cases

The Sydney and New South Wales government health data pages typically show up on the first page of search results, along with a page of Sydney COVID-19 data from Our World in Data.

**Site:** Search with a specific website for information on sublevel pages. This is particularly helpful with government websites -- and some media websites -- that have poor search engines built in. Use Google to work around it by typing this into the Google search field:

Site:[cdc.gov](https://cdc.gov) SARS

The result is a deep list of SARS-related pages useful to journalists: About page, fact sheet, FAQ, historical timeline, etc. ([Figure 2.3](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_003)).

There are many other useful search operators that Russell and others recommend:


<a href="#ch02.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch002_Figure_003"><em>Figure</em> <em>2.3</em></a> Site operator search for SARS on <a href="https://www.CDC.gov">CDC.gov</a>


- **Site:[youtube.com](https://youtube.com).** YouTube has a good search engine, of course, because Google owns it. But if you're not getting the results you want, try Google.com. Type in Site:youtube and whatever topic you're searching. Try [video.google.com](https://www.video.google.com) to search several platforms for video, including many news sites.
- **Site minus site.** A search like: \[site:[nyc.gov](https://nyc.gov) -site:[www.nyc.gov](http://www.nyc.gov)\] will give you sites in [NYC.gov](https://www.NYC.gov) that do NOT begin with WWW. That's handy for finding subdomains within a particular site, which you can then use "site:" to search.
- **Stars in site search.** A search like \[site:\*.law.\*.edu\] will find all of the. EDU sites with ".law" in the domain name. Also try: \[site:\*.[nyc.gov](http://nyc.gov)\] will match all of the [NYC.gov](https://www.NYC.gov) sites with a subdomain. Also, \[site:\*.nasa.\* inurl:education\] gives lots of good clues about education sites at NASA.
- **Quotation marks.** Using quotation marks in your search terms lets you search specifically for those words. It means all of your results will have your search terms in them. Example: "BLS unemployment data New York."
- **Hyphens.** If you want to exclude a term from your search, include a hyphen before the word. Example: If you're looking for information on dolphins and don't want to include the Miami Dolphins, try dolphins -football or dolphins -miami
- **Tilde (\~).** Use the tilde when you also want its synonyms to appear in the result. Example: Music \~classes will search for music classes, lessons, coaching, etc.
- **Vertical bar (\|).** This query will search websites that have any one/two/all of the search terms. Example: Biden \| economy \| stock market
- **Two periods (..).** Use this when you want to search within a range of two numbers. Example: DSLR camera prices \$500.. \$900
- **AROUND.** Search expert Henk van Ess teaches this operator in his Google sessions. AROUND (be sure to use all caps so Google doesn't include it in the actual search) lets you search for two terms within a range of words from each other. For example, "gdp" AROUND(20) "Ireland" gives you all results about Ireland's GDP when the two words are within 20 words of one another. With changes to Google's search algorithm in the last few years, this operator has become much more popular.
- **\@twitter keywords.** Just type \@twitter and a few keywords after it to search tweets for a specific topic. You can include locations, too, to help with coverage of local breaking news.
  - *Example:* Search \@twitter F1 race Monte Carlo in Google and it gives you accounts and tweets from the famous auto race. In my results, it listed the official race and F1 twitter accounts first, followed by links to various tweets. I've had mixed results using this search operator, but it has produced some worthwhile searches. It's worth a try on a breaking story.

Sunne often uses search operators when setting up news alerts. She sets up new Google searches via Google Alerts, or other more specific tools like Google Scholar or web-update monitors like Visualping or Distill.io.

A Google Alerts search would be something like: site:[lla.la.gov](https://lla.la.gov) investigative ("new orleans" OR "orleans parish")

The search pings Sunne when the Louisiana Legislative Auditor (with the website [lla.louisiana.gov](https://www.lla.louisiana.gov)) posts a new investigative audit that mentioned either the city of New Orleans or its surrounding county. Google Alerts aren't instantaneous -- they arrive only within a day or so of a new Google result for that search. But it is still a good way to use filters to stay on top of specific files, agencies or news topics.

Sunne also uses some paid "people searcher" sites that are powerful, but can also be costly. LexisNexis is one example. In recent years she has been using a combination of free sites, which you can find on Google, and TLO, a paid "people searcher."

::: section
## Google Advanced Search

If you aren't comfortable using search operators, you also can use Google's Advanced Search form. It's a bit hard to find: Click on the settings link in the lower right corner of [Google.com](https://www.Google.com) and select Advanced Search from the pulldown menu.

A form will appear ([Figure 2.4](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_004)) that you can fill out to accomplish many of the search operator functions without having to type in the operator itself. Simply put the words into the form for site:, filetype or, in the example in [Figure 2.4](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_004), a search for jaguar speed without a car. The search results should give you the speed for jaguar cats and not for the automobile.

You also can use the "Any of these words" field to enter specific terms in quote marks. For instance, if you're researching an earthquake in Mexico City, type: "earthquake" "sismo" "mexico city" "CDMX" into the field.

For reporters, advanced search can be a quick way to narrow down searches by region, language, number/price ranges, etc. For many, the search operators are a first option, but the Advanced Search form makes for a nice backup, especially if you can't remember the search operators.

::: section
## Other Search Options

While Google is a valuable search tool for data journalists, there are other useful tools for searching the Internet for datasets, including:

- Bing, Microsoft's search engine that has incorporated ChatGPT
- Yahoo!
- Microsoft Academic, which searches academic journals, much like Google Scholar.

Some journalists prefer Duck Duck Go or Startpage because they vow to protect your privacy by not tracking your search history. In China, which has banned Google, the most popular search engine is Baidu; in Russia, it's Yandex.


<a href="#ch02.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch002_Figure_004"><em>Figure</em> <em>2.4</em></a> Google Advanced Search interface for jaguar speed – car


\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box2" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">


When Googling, you can use the site filter with only the domain, in order to get any website on that domain. For example, "site:.gov" will return only government websites, while "site:.edu" will return only educational institutions.

Give it a try: site:.edu racial profiling

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## Google's Dataset Search Engine

In 2018, Google launched Dataset Search, which allows you to search millions of datasets posted by academic researchers, nonprofit organizations, companies and government agencies. Google's reasoning: Datasets are easier to find when they include clear and consistent supporting information, such as the data's description, source and file format. Those details can help journalists determine whether a dataset is worth downloading, thus saving hours of research time.

As you type keywords into the Dataset Search box, Google suggests possible datasets. Moreover, you can filter the research results by when the data was posted, its format and whether you must pay to download the data. Just use the filters tab at the top of the interface.

The tabs down the left side of the interface allow you to move to various datasets. As you click each tab, the description of the dataset -- provided by the data's producers -- appears on the right side of the interface. This includes the format, any special permissions (most are public record or available under Creative Commons licenses) and detail the datasets methodology.

It's important to note that Google doesn't fact-check the datasets. It merely guides you to the datasets and provides information about them so you can vet the data yourself. It's an incredibly efficient tool for finding clean datasets on deadline.

Journalists also can have their datasets listed in the search engine by completing the steps outlined on the tool's developer page.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Tracking Search in Google Trends

Journalists use Google Trends ([trends.google.com](https://www.trends.google.com)) to track what people are searching for on Google and to test SEO keywords to see how they're performing. As of June 2023, there are 8.5 billion searches per day on Google, and this database tracks them back to 2004.

The simple interface lets you compare multiple search topics at once and see how they perform in a basic line chart, and, in some cases, maps. You can look at search on a minute-by-minute basis, hourly, weekly, monthly, annually or select a period of time you want to focus on (e.g. election year).

You can do worldwide search, by country, state/province and even larger cities. This helps you drill down and see how a global search term is performing in your area, or vice versa. Let's say you are covering a story that has gone viral. You can load the search term in to see how it's performing globally, or in Pakistan, Mexico or Russia.

The pulldown menu to the right narrows your search. You can track web searches, YouTube searches, Google Shopping, Google News and more.

Trends was particularly helpful at the start of the pandemic, when you could track what people were searching for: Clorox wipes, toilet paper, surgical masks and other PPE. People also searched for symptoms.

Trends also is helpful for local and national elections. For example, let's answer the question: Who was searched more during the 2016 U.S. presidential election: Donald Trump or Hillary Clinton? Follow these steps:

1.  Open [trends.google.com](https://www.trends.google.com) and type "Hillary Clinton" in the field at the top. Make sure you spell it right; Trends doesn't account for misspellings the way Google search does.
2.   A line chart will show on the screen. Type "Donald Trump" into the "Compare" field next to Clinton's name. Make sure on both names to select "Search Term" from the pulldown menu. You also can select subtopics for context (Clinton as secretary of state, for example).
3.  In the pulldown underneath Clinton's name, you'll see it defaults to "United States." Click in the field and you can type a name or select a country, state, province or city from the pulldown menus. Type in "Chicago."
4.  In the next pulldown menu, labeled "Past Day," hit the menu arrow and select "Custom Time Range" at the bottom. Hit the radio button that says "Full year" and from the pulldown menu select 2016, the campaign year. Your graphic should appear ([Figure 2.5](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_005)).
5.  You can link to the page by copying the link in your browser field at the top. If you want to embed it, click on the button labeled "\< \>" in the upper right corner of the graphic to get JavaScript embed code. The page also will give you other embeddable charts, maps and lists of search terms from that topic and area.

::: section
## Analyzing the Chart and Data

It's easy to see in the chart that Trump dominated search during most of the campaign. He said controversial things and stole headlines. But note that Clinton surged ahead on a few dates:


<a href="#ch02.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch002_Figure_005"><em>Figure</em> <em>2.5</em></a> Google Trends 2016 search results (<em>Interactive chart</em>)


- **Week of June 5:** FBI email investigation heated up, according to a search of her name in Google News.
- **Week of July 24:** The Democratic National Convention.
- **Week of Sept. 11:** Clinton passed out at the 9/11 memorial and it made international headlines.

Be careful not to draw assumptions about those surges in traffic. I searched in Google News thoroughly for those headlines and dates before using them in a story or presentation.

You also may need to explain the Trends chart's y-axis to readers in the story, so they understand that the 0 to 100 represents a normalization, not the actual traffic numbers. This explanation can be accessed on the "?" button in the top left of the graphic. It reads:

> Numbers represent search interest relative to the highest point on the chart for the given region and time. A value of 100 is the peak popularity for the term. A value of 50 means that the term is half as popular. A score of 0 means there was not enough data for this term.

**A word of caution:** Don't draw gross generalizations from Google Trends. Just because candidates are being searched more than others doesn't mean they're more popular or are going to win the election. They might be getting searched for all the wrong reasons.

When attributing Google Trends results in a story, it's best to use: "According to an analysis of Google search data." It's clear to the reader what you're doing with the data, as many readers don't know what Trends is or does.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box3" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">


Google's Simon Rogers writes an excellent free Google Trends e-newsletter that publishes on weekdays. Sign up here: [http://goo.gle/trendsemail](http://goo.gles). Follow \@googletrends on Twitter to see what international and national topics are trending, then decide if you want to localize them for a story.

In December, Trends does a "Year in Review" that highlights the top-searched topics by genre (news, sports, entertainment, most misspelled, etc.) It's an entertaining read and can be helpful for generating end-of-the-year story ideas.

</aside>

\* \* \*

:::: section
## Using The Wayback Machine

For more than a quarter of a century, journalists have used The Internet Archive Wayback Machine ([https://archive.org/](https://archive.org)) to visit archived versions of websites, including those that have been taken down. The site creates an Internet library for researchers, historians, scholars and others, and it collaborates with many institutions, including the Library of Congress and the Smithsonian. (Fun fact: it's named after Mr. Peabody's WABAC -- pronounced "way-back" -- machine from the old "Rocky and Bullwinkle" cartoon show.)

ProPublica fact-checking guru Craig Silverman loves the "Changes" tool on the Wayback Machine. The button appears in the top toolbar. Put it to the test: Paste the link of the National Rifle Association's About page into the search field at the top of the page: [https://home.nra.org/about-the-nra](https://home.nra.org).

Then hit the "search archived websites" radio button, then the "Go" button. The "Changes" button at the top of the interface will give you calendars going back several years, including a few dates with blue marks on them. Those were dates that the page was updated. As you can see, the NRA about page was updated twice in April 2023 and twice in February 2023. This is helpful for seeing if an organization is changing or hiding something. Select two captures and then click the "Compare" button at the top of the page to see a side-by-side view of the captures.

The tool offers many other features, such as bulk archiving web addresses using Google Sheets; verifying page elements; finding "lost sites" that closed down years ago; search scholarly research; movies, software, music and more.

Similar to Google, the Wayback Machine also has an advanced search page that contains a form you can fill out to tailor your search. It also has browser extensions for most of the leading web browsers.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box4" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">


::: section
## Google Trends 

Track what people are searching in Google and create visualizations with the data:

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=p5Se4WNt1Ng](http://www.youtube.com)

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## Deep Web Resources

The "deep web" is a term for all of the data that is not indexed by search engines -- that is, not considered by Google and others as a potential search result. It includes things like Facebook profiles, records in a searchable database or the time and date of a YouTube video.

"As surprising as it may seem, the 'deep web' makes up most of the content on the web," Sunne wrote in her textbook, "Data + Journalism."

These are a few examples of the "deep web" data sources around the world that Sunne assembled for her book. As a journalist, you will probably need to find more localized datasets, which you can find through interviews and Internet searches of your own, or through digging deep with specific searches in these portals. You also can mine local data out of larger datasets by using pivot tables, sorting and filtering data in a Google Sheet or Excel.

+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| *Data Portals*                                |                                                                                                                                                                  |
+===============================================+==================================================================================================================================================================+
| [Data.gov](https://www.Data.gov)              | The United States' federal open data portal is a hub for agencies and departments from different levels of government.                                           |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| data.go.jp                                    | Many countries and states have their own open data portals. This one, from Japan, collects data from government agencies as well as some nongovernmental groups. |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| UNdata                                        | The United Nations' data hub can be filtered down by topic and country.                                                                                          |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| Court Documents                               |                                                                                                                                                                  |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| PACER                                         | PACER is the U.S. government's portal for federal court records. Federal court is different from city, state, appellate and other court systems.                 |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| International Court of Justice                | The United Nations tries war crimes in this international court, with records dating back to 1946.                                                               |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| EUR-Lex                                       | The Court of Justice of the European Union enforces laws established by the European Union, and publishes court records dating back to the 1970s.                |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| Legislation                                   |                                                                                                                                                                  |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| United Nations Treaty Series                  | This is a non-comprehensive collection of international treaties collected by the UN.                                                                            |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| Library of Congress                           | Federal U.S. legislation is tracked by this library, including bill sponsors and amendments.                                                                     |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| Financial Disclosures                                                                                                                                                                                            |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| U.S. Federal Election Commission (FEC)        | The FEC is the body responsible for tracking campaign finance disclosures for federal political campaigns in the United States.                                  |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission (SEC) | The SEC tracks financial information filed by companies that are being traded on the stock market, including their revenue and value.                            |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+
| IMF Data                                      | The International Monetary Foundation's portal includes international economic sources like the Direction of Trade Statistics.                                   |
+-----------------------------------------------+------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------+

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box5" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">


Samantha Sunne suggests that when searching an online database, you can sometimes get it to return *all* of the records by leaving the search bar blank. You can also try entering wildcards like "\*" and "%%." These tricks don't work with every database, but they are always worth trying.

</aside>

\* \* \*

:::: section
## How Sports Journalists Search

While sports journalists have many stats and facts provided for them by the universities and pro teams they cover, many times they must dig much deeper using search methods and public records.

ESPN national college football writer Adam Rittenberg has covered college football for nearly two decades. He generates stories for the web, podcasts, TV and other platforms. He files FOI requests to some of the schools to get coaching contracts and other information about elite public university programs, despite the information being of public record. He filed several for a deep-dive project ESPN did on head coach contracts.

"We wanted to get all of them from the major conference schools," he said. "I also have filed FOIs for personnel files on coaches. I haven't done many extensive court record searches, but have used some of the individual district and state websites to obtain legal documents or filings."

Rittenberg also uses a [newspapers.com](https://www.newspapers.com) account for researching more extensive projects. The archives go back several decades for most papers, even centuries, and are easy to search.

Rittenberg is fortunate to have ESPN's TruMedia database for a lot of his statistical research. He can search a library of data over specific time periods. For example, he can research the top offenses in yards per play from 2013 to 2022 with a few clicks. This is a very effective tool when putting together more extensive lists and analysis.

Even without ESPN's proprietary database, you can still easily access sports and sports business data online. A few sites that can help:

- GIJN Sports Database
- <https://bit.ly/gijnsports>
- Links to various databases and sports federations around the world.
- The Intercollegiate: Division I-A Athletic Dept. FOIA Directory
- [https://theintercollegiate.com/d-i-foia-directory](https://theintercollegiate.com)
- Find the FOIA contact for college sports programs.
- Sports Reference
- [www.sports-reference.com](http://www.sports-reference.com)
- Official statistics for pro and college sports teams that you can download as a spreadsheet in seconds. It includes some attendance and non-player data as well.
- Spotrac
- [www.spotrac.com](http://www.spotrac.com)
- Spotrac has become the largest online sports team and player contract resource on the Internet. The site has financial information for MLB, the NBA, NFL, NHL and MLS. Some of the league player unions also have accessible salary databases.
- Sportico: The Business of Sports
- [www.sportico.com/c/business/finance](http://www.sportico.com)
- The finance section has many helpful datasets. Requires a subscription. Covers sports all over the world.
- Stathead
- <https://stathead.com>
- Used by analytically minded journalists, front offices and fans, Stathead is the industry standard for finding and analyzing the statistical history of your favorite sport.
- Statista: Sports on U.S. TV
- [www.statista.com/topics/2113/sports-on-tv/#topicOverview](http://www.statista.com)
- This paid site has TV ratings over the years on several sports events, including the Super Bowl.
- StatMuse
- [www.statmuse.com](http://www.statmuse.com)
- Search players, stats, records, odds and more. Includes a data glossary.

\* \* \*

## Video: Finding Data 

How to use international and local data portals to track down datasets.

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=czd3Vs84xhk](http://www.youtube.com)

::: section
## Hacking a Web Address to Find Archived Data

Many times, government agencies don't maintain an easy-to-navigate archive of their records on the web, providing only the most current dataset. To find older datasets, you can hack the web address of the most-recent dataset as agencies have consistent URLs for naming files.

Sunne experiments with URLs to see what is buried deep on a website. She figured if Google can search within a domain like *site:[fbi.gov/news](https://fbi.gov)/*, meaning the News section of the FBI's website, she could move around within those URLs as well.

She experimented with the U.S. Department of Defense for a story she was working on for Reuters. To her surprise, she was able to manually change URLs and find new information.

How she did it: Simply change the ID or the section in a URL to pull up a document she was looking for. For instance, the DOD uploaded reports on its website with a format like, "[www.defense.gov/reports/loc_531/20190430/](http://www.defense.gov)." This meant the report was filed on April 30, 2019, for a location with the ID "loc_531." If she changed the URL to read "20180430," it would show her a report from that same location for the same day a year earlier. She could also change locations if she knew the ID number, which she easily found online.

"This method worked better than searching the DOD's website," Sunne said. "It was an interesting exercise in how much you can circumnavigate a website's structure, and also how much you can stumble upon online."

:::: section
## Exercise: Hack a URL

The U.S. Postal Service (USPS) publishes its quarterly performance report on its website. The data table shows the percentage of mail delivered on time in each postal district. The page of quarter 3 data from 2020 is shown in [Figure 2.6](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_006).

Here is the website address for that table. Note that the fiscal year and quarter are boldfaced:

- [https://about.usps.com/what/performance/service-performance/**fy2020**-**q3**-single-piece-first-class-mail-quarterly-performance.html](https://about.usps.com)

To find quarter 4 data, simply change the q3 in the URL to q4:

- [https://about.usps.com/what/performance/service-performance/**fy2020**-**q4**-single-piece-first-class-mail-quarterly-performance.html](https://about.usps.com)

You also can change the year and the quarter to find more pages:

- [https://about.usps.com/what/performance/service-performance/**fy2021**-**q1**-single-piece-first-class-mail-quarterly-performance.html](https://about.usps.com)

This can be an effective way to sort through government sites and other poorly navigated websites to find data buried deep in the archives.

\* \* \*


<a href="#ch02.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch002_Figure_006"><em>Figure</em> <em>2.6</em></a> USPS performance data table


<aside aria-label="box7" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Searching for Free Tools 

Many times, journalists working in the field may not have software loaded on their computers to complete a task. Finding browser-based tools to complete the task can be time-consuming, especially on deadline.

In a pinch, you can go to [AlternativeTo.net](http://AlternativeTo.net) and type in the name of some software and it will give you free (and some fee-based) software that are similar, along with reviews from users. For example, if you type in "Adobe Photoshop" in the search field, you'll get some real gems, like PhotoPea and Pixlr. Give it a try.

</aside>

::::::: section


## Exercises

::: section
### Exercise 1: Site Search Operator

Let's get some practice with search operators. The U.S. Bureau of Labor Statistics site can be difficult to navigate. Let the search operators do the work for you.

Let's say you're doing a story on rising food costs and need to look up some products and their price history.

Type this into Google: site:[bls.gov](https://bls.gov) chuck roast

You should land on some pages that list tables of food prices (national averages, by region, etc.) that you can open to find your data.

**Now try these:**

- site:[bls.gov](https://bls.gov) New York unemployment
- Site:[cdc.gov](https://cdc.gov) bird flu
- Site:[fbi.gov](https://fbi.gov) homicides

In each case, you should land on robust search results that include links to pages and tables with information you're seeking.

::: section
### Exercise 2: Filetype Search Operator

Let's try another search operator -- filetype -- to look for data on U.S. mass shootings.

Type this into Google: filetype:xlsx us mass shootings

Your result should be a robust list of datasets linked off your first search page. Typically, Mother Jones and Stanford Libraries, two of the best databases, show up in this search.

Now try the "filetype: search" on some data with a different format (doc, ppt or pdf) for a topic closer to your area of coverage. See what results you get.

As a fallback to the "filetype: search operator," try the Google Dataset Search tool to check your work: <https://datasetsearch.research.google.com>

\* \* \*

::: section
### Exercise 3: Google Trends

Use Google Trends to test keywords for SEO and headlines. Let's say you're a sports reporter writing about the University of Nebraska football team. Cornhuskers and Huskers are used interchangeably by the media, but which is best for the headline?

Google Trends tracks what people are searching for on Google. You can filter it down to a country, state and many cities. You can also filter it over the past 20 years or the past year, month, week, day or even hour. Many reporters and editors use it to test keywords. Give it a try:

Go to [trends.google.com](https://www.trends.google.com) and type "Cornhuskers" in the search field at the top.

Type "Huskers" in the "Add Comparison" field to the right

Change the Time drop-down menu to Past 7 Days (or another setting)

You should find that Huskers are searched much more than Cornhuskers (see [Figure 2.7](#ch02.xhtml__15031-5909_ch002_Figure_007)). This would make it a better fit for the headline (and it's shorter). Both words would serve well as keywords in the story's metadata.

\* \* \*


<a href="#ch02.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch002_Figure_007"><em>Figure</em> <em>2.7</em></a> Google Trends results for Cornhuskers and Huskers


::: section
### Exercise 4: Twitter Search

Here, we will find a news-relevant tweet with Twitter's advanced search syntax. The site has its own advanced search page, but it is available only to users logged in to their Twitter accounts.

In a web browser, type in ["twitter.com/whitehouse](https://www.“twitter.com)." You do not need to have a Twitter account.

- In the search bar, type "from:whitehouse 'build back better act' since: 2020--01--01."
- Click on "Latest" in the toolbar, below the search bar, to sort the tweets in chronological order.

This search takes advantage of three advanced search operators. "From:" limits the results to only tweets posted by the U.S. White House's official Twitter account. The quotes around the phrase "build back better act" limits the tweets to ones mentioning this exact phrase, as opposed to individual mentions of "build" or "act." And the date filter "since:" limits the tweets to ones posted after the date in the search.

Twitter offers a full list of search operators on its support site.

\* \* \*

::: section

# 3 Fact-Checking and Building Trust

DOI: [10.4324/9781003431787-4](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003431787-4){aria-label="D.O.I. link to this document."}


**Key Resources**

- AFP Digital Training Courses: [https://digitalcourses.afp.com/](https://digitalcourses.afp.com)
- Short videos and courses on the latest digital investigative tools and techniques.
- Bellingcat Guide to Social Media Verification: [www.bellingcat.com/resources/2021/11/01/a-beginners-guide-to-social-media-verification](http://www.bellingcat.com)
- Google Fact Check Explorer: [https://toolbox.google.com/factcheck/explorer](https://toolbox.google.com)
- Google Reverse Image Search: [https://images.google.com/](https://images.google.com)
- Verification Handbook: [https://datajournalism.com/read/handbook/verification-1](https://datajournalism.com)
- This book, created for journalists by journalists, features case studies and examples of how to verify photos, video and other news. It's free online and required reading for any journalism class.

\* \* \*

> A media environment where everything is true and nothing is true -- and the capacity to disseminate misinformation, wild conspiracy theories, to paint the opposition in wildly negative light without any rebuttal -- *that* has accelerated in ways that much more sharply polarize the electorate and make it very difficult to have a common conversation.
>
> -- Barack Obama

In August 2022, Deepak Adhikari and his team launched [NepalCheck.org](https://www.NepalCheck.org), a fact-checking platform in the South Asia country. They're tasked with identifying and debunking false information and providing readers with verified details with appropriate context. It's a model that news organizations, nonprofits and other organizations around the world have adopted with the rise of misinformation and disinformation over the past decade.


<em>Figure</em> <em>3.1</em> The NepalCheck website in 2023


With November 2022 general elections in mind, NepalCheck worked to hold politicians, public and elected officials and political party leaders to account for their false, misleading and half-truth statements through an act of fact-checking.

The team launched a website, which publishes in both English and Nepali languages, as well as Twitter and Facebook accounts.

Fact-checking organizations and news outlets around the world have banded together to build fact-checking databases on politics, the Ukraine War, COVID-19 and many other issues. Ranging from hundreds of staffers to just a few, they work tirelessly to flag inaccurate information, fake news, deep fakes and rumors and provide context to breaking news stories.

"I think two factors have determined the trajectories of modern fact-checking," Adhikari said. "First, the election of Donald Trump was a major event for the fact-checking community.

"Second, the Cambridge Analytica data scandal and the Russian interference via Facebook and other social media platforms exposed how malign actors can use these platforms. It led to the Congressional grilling of Facebook's CEO Mark Zuckerberg. It also forced Facebook to address the issue, leading to the expansion of the tech giant's third-party fact-checking program."

Sites like the Pulitzer Prize-winning PolitiFact, AFP Fact Check and Craig Silverman's work with ProPublica and Buzzfeed News, have transformed news coverage in, as Obama said, an environment where "everything is true and nothing is true."

In this chapter, you'll learn to use many of the tools Adhikari and his team use to debunk misinformation and disinformation. You'll learn steps to slow the spread of fake news, how to detect video deep fakes and understand the challenges fact-checkers face on a daily basis.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box1" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Glossary 

It's important when fact-checking to know the difference between misinformation and disinformation. The fundamental difference is intent.

- **Misinformation:** The person shares the information without knowing that it is wrong. The person believes it. Example: Your Uncle Bob shares a magic cure for COVID-19 on his Facebook page: Gargle with salt water. "We're all saved. Pandemic over!" he writes. Until he learns that's not the case.
- **Disinformation:** The person shares the bad information knowing that it is false. It's a malicious act to mislead others.

\* \* \*

</aside>

For Adhikari, fact-checking comes down to time and resources. Before launching NepalCheck, he spent 2 1/2 years as editor of South Asia Check, where he was frustrated by the fact that he didn't have the tools and staff to do real-time fact-checking.

"I wanted to fact-check a prominent politician's speech in real-time," he said. "But I soon realized that given our small team of four people and lack of databases and credible facts online, it was impossible to do it. I had read about real-time fact-checking, though I hadn't watched it live.

He had come close in February 2021, when Prime Minister KP Sharma Oli delivered a speech in Kathmandu. He reviewed the fact-checks of Oli's interviews and speeches on South Asia Check's website. Politicians often repeat the same false claims several times, so Adhikari was expecting Oli to repeat already-debunked false claims.

"I watched his speech and quickly identified several claims for a fact-check report," he said. "Oli concluded his speech around 4 p.m. I researched, reported, and published the fact-check at 6 p.m, two hours after his speech.

"It was published in English two days later \[they first publish fact-checks in Nepali\]. I fact-checked three claims Oli made in his speech that day and added one more from his earlier speech. EKantipur, Nepal's largest digital outlet, published the fact-check."

He also debunked conspiracy theories circulating among Nepali Facebook users that were picked up by several Nepal digital outlets. They were also complex issues to tackle, but after talking to experts and conducting a digital investigation, he debunked them.

"I still think we as fact-checkers need to be clear about what we know and what we don't," he said. "At the time, the science around the COVID-19 pandemic was still evolving. Confronting such a huge crisis and lack of knowledge about it, the best possible way was to acknowledge the challenges."

Sophie Nicholson, the deputy chief editor of digital investigation with AFP in Paris, helps oversee the global team, working closely with tech companies, media organizations, researchers and fact-checkers. Unlike NepalCheck, Nicholson's team of more than 140 digital investigation experts produces, on average, more than 150 fact-checks per week. In 2022 alone, AFP did 8,000 fact-checks.

"The claims we check are more often about images than just text but they can also originate from a political speech, for example," said Nicholson, who previously worked as a foreign correspondent in Mexico City and has reported from more than 15 countries.

Her fact-checkers monitor social media posts and comments sections and respond to tips and questions from the public. They work closely with more than 1,700 AFP journalists in 151 countries.

"Our strength is that we can conduct investigations on the ground and online in a complementary way," she said. "The news agency works in six languages but the fact-checking team publishes in 26 languages so the focus can be more local and we can quickly spot trends that emerge in multiple countries too.

"We work with social media platforms to help surface false claims that are spreading rapidly. This can help us to quickly identify potential misinformation to focus on, particularly if it is harmful, such as damaging supposed health remedies or false statements by politicians, and being shared by many people. Automated tools from platforms also match articles to misinformation that is being repeated to enable fact-checkers to work faster."

Nicholson and Adhikari see a major challenge to fact-checkers: AI and the Increasingly sophisticated tools to create false content.

"Deep fakes are improving and becoming much easier to produce," Nicholson said. "The scale of false information online is mind-boggling. It is time-consuming to investigate accounts that deliberately spread false information, and that is increasingly quick and easy to produce.

"Most platforms don't provide tools to help researchers and journalists do this efficiently and transparently. There is little or no fact-checking on most closed networks or on new social networks."

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box2" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Adhikari's and Nicholson's Fact-Checking Toolboxes 

- **Archived web pages:** The Wayback Machine helps both search contents of deleted websites and saves pages for future reference and record.
- **Deep Fake video:** Invid, developed by AFP and Deutsche Welle. Invid breaks a video into keyframes, which can be searched using reverse image search techniques. This saves a lot of time for fact-checkers.
- **Metadata:** Two more tools to get metadata, which help the fact-checkers determine the date of the photos or video uploaded.
- **Geolocation:** Google Earth is another tool that fact-checkers can use to pinpoint locations, as well as Google Maps and Google Earth Street View. SunCalc helps determine the date based on the analysis of shadows. The best example of this tool being used is BBC News Africa's groundbreaking investigation called Anatomy of a Killing. (More detail on these tools and the fact-check in [Chapter 8](#ch08.xhtml__15031-5909_ch008))
- **Reverse Image Search:** Google Images, [TinEye.com](https://www.TinEye.com) and the RevEye Chrome extension let you reverse search the web for images to learn where and when they were first posted. There are several exercises using these tools near the end of this chapter.

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## Fact-Checking and the Rise of Fake News

As Adhikari noted earlier, the 2016 U.S. presidential election was a landmark moment in fact-checking. Trump galvanized fact-checkers looking for misinformation and, in many cases, disinformation. The election also marked a surge in fake news sites that spread misinformation and disinformation over social media channels. Some were simply propaganda to generate influence over voters and election results. Others wanted commercial gain through advertising due to the large traffic numbers that came to the site.

In his book, "The Online Journalism Handbook" (second edition), Paul Bradshaw wrote about how Facebook took most of the criticism over fake news, though Google, Twitter and other platforms also shouldered blame.

Early in 2016, Facebook had fired the human editors overseeing its "Trending Topics" section that had led to fake stories appearing in that section within days, Bradshaw wrote. Its feed algorithms gave less weight to established publishers and brands. Bradshaw noted that one Buzzfeed investigation into more than 1,000 posts on hyperpartisan Facebook pages found that "the least accurate pages generated some of the highest numbers of shares, reactions and comments on Facebook." They had more engagement than mainstream news pages.

By November, Zuckerberg had announced plans to tackle fake news through better reporting and detection systems.

Google followed suit by attaching fact-checks to stories in its Google New search, which eventually led to the Google Fact Check Explorer tool that allows users to search fact-checks by person or topic.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Debunking Fake News Stories

You can save yourself a lot of grief and embarrassment by following a few simple steps before sharing a story or quoting from it.

- **Think before sharing and read beyond the story's headline.** Never retweet, like or share anything until you've clicked the link and read the story, even if it comes from a familiar, reliable source. Apply critical thinking skills: Does anything not seem right with the piece? Then like or share it. Most social media tools now stop you and ask if you want to click on the link before sharing.
- **Search if other news outlets are reporting it.** This is one of the first things I do after reading the story, especially if it involves a death or someone is accused of something. Can I confirm the story through another news outlet?
- **Check for fact-checks.** There are hundreds of fact-checking organizations around the world. Google the topic or a person from the story. Look at [Snopes.com](https://www.Snopes.com), PolitiFact or another fact-checking site to see if it's part of a prank or disinformation campaign. Search the topic or person in the Google Fact Check Explorer. Both are quick ways to avoid the pain of getting something wrong.
- **Check the news outlet that published it and who the author is.** Are you familiar with the outlet and the author? Check their social media channels. What are they sharing? How are they engaging readers? Many times fact-checkers will debunk a fake news story in Twitter replies, Facebook or story comments. Take the time to look for them.
- **Look at the publish date and time.** How old is the story? From today, yesterday, a week or years ago? Sometimes people will share stories on social media in the context of breaking news, when the story occurred days, weeks or even years ago. Check the date; when a story happened matters.
- **Look at what links and sources are used in the article.** Is the information attributed to an on-the-record source? To a study? How current is the data? Is the story linking to credible examples, reports, public records, etc.? If the links don't work or you don't see any attribution, chances are you may be looking at a fake news piece.
- **Look for questionable quotes and photos.** Remember, photos and words together don't equal facts. Pursue the truth. Look closely at the photos: Are the person's eyes, ears and hands disproportionate (a key sign for AI-generated images). Do searches on the images (which you'll learn how to do later in this chapter). Copy the quote and Google it: Where has it been previously published? Maybe the author stole it from another story or made it up.
- **Beware of confirmation bias.** This is more for political or ideological stories, but make sure your own beliefs aren't interfering with your ability to seek the truth in the story. Even if you disagree with what some sources are saying in a story about abortion rights, it doesn't mean what they're saying is inaccurate. Check yourself and make sure to approach stories on controversial issues with an open mind.

\* \* \*


::: section
## Fact-Checking in Sports Coverage

ESPN national college football writer Adam Rittenberg started at ESPN in 2008 and has seen many changes in the industry -- and in his workflow -- over the past 16 years. While the web has made his job easier, it also has made it more challenging because of the flow of misinformation.

"Information is much more accessible now, but I also have to be more diligent about sourcing and checking certain things," said Rittenberg, who produces an annual series called "Future Power Rankings," which is a three-year projection of the top 25 offenses, defenses, quarterback situations and overall teams in college football.

"The final piece eclipses 10,000 words, and would take me much longer to put together if I couldn't access projected depth charts, recruiting rankings and an array of stories. The speed where information flows is also a big change, especially with social media."

\* \* \*

:::: section
## Fact-Checking Images

When fact-checking images, First Draft New, the former fact-checking organization at Harvard, recommends following these tips:

- **Provenance:** Are you looking at the original piece of content? Give the image the "eye test" -- does it look altered in any way?
- **Source:** Who captured the original piece of content? You can find this through a reverse image search.
- **Date:** When was the piece of content captured? You also can verify this through an image search.
- **Location:** Where was the piece of content captured? In [Chapter 8](#ch08.xhtml__15031-5909_ch008), we explore how to use the Google Earth suite and other tools to confirm a photo's location.
- **Motivation:** Why was the piece of content captured? Look at the context in which it's presented, the reputation of the organization or person sharing it. Is the photo from a journalist documenting a protest, or from a protester's perspective? Maybe it's a photo from an eyewitness, or a police officer documenting what's happening for legal reasons. Who is shooting it and why is important for providing the reader with context, or determining if it's valid.

I always encourage students to give the photo an "eye test." For example, if someone you don't know tweeted a photo to you of several cars submerged or nearly submerged in a Missouri flood from today, you'd look over the photo closely. What if the area looked more urban, like New York City? What if the license plates on the cars all said New York? It's highly improbable that the photo was taken in Missouri. You could check weather reports and see if there was even flooding in Missouri. Look for news stories. Check [Snopes.com](https://www.Snopes.com) and a couple of fact-checking sites.

Then, to be sure, you could move the image into Google Image Search, TinEye, Bing or another tool to see where and when the photo was first published. If it shows up today in Missouri, you're good. If it shows up as far back as a year ago in New York state, you're being duped by someone.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box3" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Image and Video Fact-Checking Tools 

- **Google Image Search:** <https://images.google.com>
- Reverse image search a photo in this tool or [TinEye.com](https://www.TinEye.com) to see when and where it was originally published. These tools are excellent for confirming the context of a photo given to you is accurate.
- **Search This Image on Twitter:** You also can follow the \@searchthisimage bot on Twitter. When you see a photo in a tweet you want searched, just reply under the tweet with \@searchthisimage and wait for a bit. The bot should reply to the tweet with a link to the reverse image search results. This works particularly well on mobile devices, where reverse image search on Google can be a bit clumsy.
- **FotoForensics:** <https://fotoforensics.com>
- Breaks down metadata and layers on a site. Run the tutorial linked off the bottom of the page to see how it works.
- **Forensically:** [https://29a.ch/photo-forensics/#forensic-magnifier](https://29a.ch)
- Similar to FotoForensics, it breaks down metadata and other parts of photos for fact-checking.
- **IPVM:** <https://calculator.ipvm.com>
- Image forensics tool. Use to fact-check images by confirming locations.
- **[WatchFramebyFrame.com](https://www.WatchFramebyFrame.com):** [www.watchframebyframe.com](http://www.watchframebyframe.com)
- Great for fact-checking videos. Paste the URL into the player and hit the arrow keys to look at each shot frame by frame. Watch for shadows out of place, etc. You can watch frame by frame on YouTube by using the comma and period keys after pausing the video. The comma key moves the video backward, and the period key moves it forward.

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## How to Do a Reverse Image Search

When I do image searches, I use Google Image Search and TinEye interchangeably. They run different algorithms and provide similar, but sometimes different, results. A good tool to add to your Chrome browser is RevEye, which lets you reverse image search a photo over five search tools (including Google and TinEye) by simply right-clicking on it and selecting the Reverse image search option.

In [images.google.com](https://www.images.google.com), you can search an image three ways:

1.  Right-click on the image (Control + click on a Mac) on the page it's published and select "Search Images with Google" in the pulldown menu ([Figure 3.2](#ch03.xhtml__15031-5909_ch003_Figure_002)). It will either take you to a page of images or ask you to select an area of the photo you want to search (drag your cursor across the entire photo).
2.  Then click on the "Find Image Source" button on the interface. Up will pop a series of stories where the photo has appeared ([Figure 3.3](#ch03.xhtml__15031-5909_ch003_Figure_003)). Scroll through them to find the earliest posts and see where the photo first appeared. There's also a Tools button at the top of [images.google.com](https://www.images.google.com) that allows you to filter by date, file format and other parameters.

You also can paste a link to a photo into [images.google.com](https://www.images.google.com) or drag and drop an image off your desktop to search it. [TinEye.com](https://www.TinEye.com) also lets you paste URLs into the interface and right-click on photos.

If you think the photo might be doctored, you can try a more advanced tool like [FotoForensics.com](https://www.FotoForensics.com) to check its metadata and see if the photo has been layered and flattened/compressed as a. jpg. This takes some work, but a short tutorial in FotoForensics walks you through it in about five minutes.


<a href="#ch03.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch003_Figure_002"><em>Figure</em> <em>3.2</em></a> Google reverse image search pulldown menu


<a href="#ch03.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch003_Figure_003"><em>Figure</em> <em>3.3</em></a> Google reverse image search results


You'll have the opportunity to put your photo and video fact-checking skills to the test in exercises at the end of this chapter.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Ethics and Building Trust With Readers

Lauren Camplin took five journalism courses with me when she was a student at DePaul University. Ethics were part of every class she took with me, whether it was newswriting, online journalism, sports reporting, news editing, etc. I baked the SPJ and NPPA Codes of Ethics into Photoshop training. I examined case studies on airing suicide details or footage of a stunt performer falling to his death in a public square in Seattle. I talked about real-life coverage of local news stories.

Many students in those classes groused and rolled their eyes when handed an ethics code, but Camplin never did. She was always thoughtful and showed good judgment while standing her ground at sometimes unpopular decisions in class discussions.

Upon graduation, she scored a job as an overnight producer at ESPN. She was promoted to the day shift in 2013 and her first day on the job was April 15, Patriot's Day. The day of the Boston Marathon.

A day later, I received this email:

> *From: Lauren Camplin*
>
> *Sent: Tuesday, April 16, 2013 10:40 AM*
>
> *To: Reilley, Michael*
>
> *Subject: Boston*
>
> *Hey*,
>
> *Hope you're doing well. Just wanted to shoot you a note saying thanks for all the ethical teaching you did in classes because it definitely helped me yesterday*.
>
> *I was doing media for the 3 p.m. SportsCenter when the bombs went off and was solely responsible for all the video, sound, etc. we ran for about four straight hours since we dropped commercial breaks*.
>
> *You sit in a classroom thinking you won't have to deal with those kinds of situations and make those calls but it really comes in handy. So, thanks! Hope DePaul is treating you well -- and your students too!*
>
> *Lauren Camplin*

Ethics training might not be the sexiest skill you hone during college. But, as Camplin well knows, it's pretty handy when you're on the hotseat overseeing live coverage of a terrorist bombing.

Like many journalists, Camplin faced some tough decisions that day. Does she allow the live feed of the bombing victims on air before the families had been notified? The Society of Professional Journalists' Code of Ethics tells us to minimize harm. Should she airtight shots camera operators took of the devastation? Ethic codes tell us not to pander to lurid curiosity and to consider the harm it may cause victims and their families.

Ethics decisions come in many shades of gray. There are absolutes -- don't accept money and gifts from sources, don't alter news photos, etc. But many times ethics boil down to not just what we have the right to do legally as a journalist, but what's the *right thing* to do.

::: section
## Codes of Ethics

The Reynolds Journalism Institute created the Accountable Journalism database of more than 400 searchable codes of ethics from media outlets around the world:

Here are some of the best:

- National Press Photographers Association: [https://nppa.org/code-ethics](https://nppa.org)
- Radio TV Digital News Association: [www.rtdna.org/content/rtdna_code_of_ethics](http://www.rtdna.org)

- New York Times Ethical Journalism Handbook: [www.nytimes.com/editorial-standards/ethical-journalism.html](http://www.nytimes.com)
- National Public Radio Ethics: [www.npr.org/ethics](http://www.npr.org)
- Al Jazeera Code of Ethics: [http://america.aljazeera.com/tools/code-of-ethics.html](http://america.aljazeera.com)
- The Guardian's Editorial Code: [www.theguardian.com/info/2015/aug/05/the-guardians-editorial-code](http://www.theguardian.com)
- BBC Code of Conduct: [www.bbc.com/aboutthebbc/reports/policies/codeofconduct](http://www.bbc.com)
- Both employment handbook and ethics.
- CNN Code of Ethics: [https://accountablejournalism.org/ethics-codes/telling-the-world-stories](https://accountablejournalism.org)

::: section
## Trusting News

Fact-checking efforts have accelerated at a time when trust in news by audiences have reached historic lows. According to a 2022 Gallup survey, only one in three Americans trust mass media to report the news "fully, accurately and fairly." Only 7% of Americans have "a great deal" of trust and confidence in the media, and 27% have "a fair amount." Meanwhile, 28% of U.S. adults say they do not have very much confidence and 38% have none at all in newspapers, TV and radio.

In that same study, Americans were 17 percentage points more likely to say they trust reporting by local news organizations "a great deal" or "quite a lot" than to trust reporting by national news organizations.

Trust numbers in the United States have been on a steady decline since the mid-1970s, when trust hovered at 75%. The current U.S. numbers are significantly lower than in other countries, such as China, where trust levels hover near 80%.

While trust may be higher in local news, a 2022 research project the Radio Television Digital News Association (RTDNA) conducted with Magid shows local news is not exempt from the factors that lead to distrust. According to the research, "if just one journalist from a local news source is not trusted, audiences are overwhelmingly less likely to use the source overall."

When RTDNA and Magid leaders released the study, they led a discussion at the RTDNA national conference about solutions for building trust:

- **Be Right:** News consumers trust journalists when they explain how stories developed and changed over time.
- **Be Your Community:** News consumers trust journalists who are knowledgeable about the local community and what issues are important to them.
- **Be Real:** News consumers trust journalists when they are authentic, genuine and own up to a mistake.
- **Be Neutral:** News consumers trust journalists who leave personal opinions out of news coverage.

\* \* \*


:::::::: section
## Exercises

It's time to put your fact-checking skills to the test. Here are three assignments that will help you master checking videos, photos, spot fake social accounts:

\* \* \*

::: section
### Exercise 1: Spot the Troll

Think you are good at spotting a troll account on social media? Let's put this to the test. Take this online quiz from Clemson's Media Lab and see if you can determine which social media accounts are real and which are fake.

<https://spotthetroll.org>

The quiz is graded as you go and the tool explains what to look for with fake accounts.

\* \* \*

:::: section
### Exercise 2: Photos

The three photos in the folder linked below are real photos and have been shared extensively online. I'll give you the "context" in which the photos have been shared (in the following list), and you confirm whether they have or have not been represented accurately. Find the files in this folder: <https://bit.ly/photofactcheck>

1.  **Grande.jpg:** This photo is from the aftermath of the explosion at Manchester Arena during the Ariana Grande concert and was shared widely on social channels. She appears to have blood on her. Was she injured? Was the photo from that night? If not, where was it from?
2.  **ClimateStrikeProtest2019.jpg:** This photo was shared on social media heavily after the Sept. 20 Climate Strike protests around the world. It shows an area trashed by protesters after their demonstration. Not good for the climate.
3.  **FrozenChicago.jpg:** This photo is of a homeless man frozen on the streets of Chicago during the February 2019 Polar Vortex. It was heavily shared by Democrats and Republicans on social media to argue political points.

*The Fact-Check:* Use [images.google.com](https://www.images.google.com) or [TinEye.com](https://www.TinEye.com) to reverse image search the three images and confirm each photo. Are they from the news event people imply they are from? Have your staff cite sources and give details in their answers.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box4" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Fact-Checking Videos 

Learn how to use Watch Frame-by-Frame to break down videos.

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=K1yKTwK1k00](http://www.youtube.com)

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
### ***Exercise 3: Fact-Check Video***

This video made news when it circulated on the web. It's of a "golden eagle" swooping down to pick up a small boy in a Montreal park. In reality, it was made by Montreal film students playing with green screens.

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=DPXAK2lJmEQ](http://www.youtube.com)

How to tell whether it's fake? Paste the video link into [WatchFramebyFrame.com](https://www.WatchFramebyFrame.com): [www.watchframebyframe.com](http://www.watchframebyframe.com). Then hit the arrow keys to look at each shot frame-by-frame. Watch for shadows out of place, going in various directions. They don't match the time of day/sun's trajectory. That is where you can see it has been doctored.

You also can watch frame-by-frame in YouTube by using the comma and period keys after pausing the video. Comma moves backward, and period, forward. Test both out with this famous fake (watch shadows of bird/kid).

\* \* \*

::: section
### ***Exercises Answer Key***

- **Exercise 1:** The photo isn't of Grande from the Manchester bombing. Rather, it is a shot from her on the set of the TV show "Scream Queens". The blood on her is fake.
- **Exerciset 2:** The photo isn't from a climate strike protest but from a marijuana festival years earlier in London.
- **Exercise 3:** The photo isn't from Chicago during the 2019 Polar Vortex. It was shot in Toronto several years earlier.
- **Video assignment:** The video isn't real. It was created by a group of Montreal film students against a green screen. Slowing down the frames of the video shows the shadows of the bird and boy don't match the angles of other shadows in the video.
