# 4 Social Media

DOI: [10.4324/9781003431787-5](https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003431787-5){aria-label="D.O.I. link to this document."}


**Key Resource Pages**

- JournalistsToolbox.ai: <http://journaliststoolbox.ai>
- Troll-Busters: [www.troll-busters.com](http://www.troll-busters.com)
- TRFilter: [www.trfilter.org](http://www.trfilter.org)
- Twitter Analytics: <https://analytics.twitter.com>

\* \* \*

In 2016, USC associate professor Amara Aguilar and eight Annenberg School for Communication and Journalism students founded Dímelo, a storytelling platform where Latinx students could see themselves and their communities better represented in news coverage from other student media outlets at the school.

Dímelo, which means "tell me" in Spanish, started as a Facebook page but quickly expanded to other social media platforms -- Instagram, YouTube, Spotify -- as well as websites on Medium and Annenberg Media. Their most popular platform is Snapchat, where they have about 185,000 subscribers on Discover.

Aguilar said Dímelo's goal is to reach young Latinx and Hispanic adults with stories and content experiences that matter to them.

"Students originally wanted to start a Spanish language broadcast, but as we talked through the audience they wanted to reach, it became apparent the goals needed to shift to meet the target audience of young Latinx adults where they were consuming news," Aguilar said.

Prior to the emergence of social media, news traveled pretty much on a one-way street. It came from one entity, person or organization to a larger group, which tended to be a passive audience. Even with reader comments and newsgroups emerging in the mid-1990s on news sites, traditional news media still preached "content is king" with an "if you build it, they will come" approach to audiences.

But the audience responded by seeking *community* online. Places like Reddit, various social sharing apps, hashtags, Facebook group pages and other platforms gave people a sense of belonging. Suddenly, news became a discussion beyond a letter to the editor.

In this chapter, you'll explore how to build content for social channels, reach an audience, cover breaking news over social media, crowdsource user-generated content to enhance reporting and deal with online harassment. You'll also learn about third-party tools and apps that enhance social media reporting, and how to study social media analytics.

::: section
## Building an Audience

Developing an audience over social channels isn't done overnight. It takes time and study. Dímelo covers community stories, including those in Los Angeles, but also focuses on content that goes beyond boundaries -- beyond geographic borders, cultural borders, stereotypes and beyond the surface of important issues that affect our world.

"We had the students do extensive research on the audience they wanted to reach, and also examine their own habits and community," Aguilar said. "At the time, media organizations like mitú were also popular and the students were inspired.

"We are also expanding tremendously in the area of podcasts and specifically on Spotify. The beauty of Dímelo is we are a small team and can be nimble and creative. We can try experiments and new story formats. The content is also very personal and genuine. We've never turned a pitch down at Dímelo, which is quite different than you might find in most newsrooms."

Dímelo publishes in English and Spanish and aims to amplify unheard voices, engage audiences, create community and focus on issues that diverse audiences care about. Topics we focus on include politics, family, culture, diversity, food, sports, education, entertainment, humor, lifestyles, empowerment and more.

"Dímelo also goes beyond borders," Aguilar said. "Our stories also include what is happening in our Latin American countries. We strive to end with stereotypes that have affected the Latinx community.

"Because we publish in English and Spanish, our message is greater by covering unheard voices and publishing for diverse audiences in a multimedia platform."

Dímelo started as a directed research class that Aguilar co-taught in 2016. In the two years Aguilar taught the class with Laura Castañeda, student demand increased. The course evolved into a permanent class called Social Media Storytelling for Latino Audiences.

The class evolved as the project grew. It originally served as a place for instruction, newsroom meetings in which students pitched ideas for each edition, and a place for creativity and innovation. Dímelo now operates independently of the course, but sometimes collaborates with the course when it is offered.

"There were many challenges at USC in creating a team and course focused on Latinos, however our student editors made their compelling case to administrators and from then on the platform and class were fully supported by the school," Aguilar said. "Now, Dímelo has grown into a robust student team supported by Annenberg.

"The school has helped us flourish through its support of curriculum -- we now have a minor in Latinx media -- and are expanding to launch a hybrid full-semester study abroad program in Mexico, among other initiatives. Since Dímelo launched within Annenberg Media, we also have had two more amazing teams launch: ElevAsian and Black."

Dímelo has published two weekly editions: Dímelo Ahora and Dímelo Discover. Both of them can be found on the USC Dímelo Snapchat account. (Launch Snapchat Discover and search for USC Dímelo). Dímelo Ahora is focused on covering news related to politics, immigration, economics or social issues that impact the Latinx community in the United States or Latin America. Dímelo Discover covers lifestyle topics such as food, music, sports, trends or entertainment.

\* \* \*


::: section
## Build Instagram Stories with Canva

Aguilar shows you how to use templates and images in Canva to build intriguing Instagram stories. You can build with a free account or upgrade to a paid account (around \$120) for more design templates and images.

[www.youtube.com/watch?v=leqfyU7oIWE](http://www.youtube.com)

\* \* \*

::: section
## Developing Social Audiences at Large Newsrooms

Aguilar and her students quickly found a niche audience with young Latinos through social media. It can be even more challenging with large legacy media outlets that have embraced one or two approaches to audiences over decades. In the last several years, roles such as "audience engagement editor" have emerged in newsrooms with the sole purpose of finding and nurturing new digital audiences.


<em>Figure</em> <em>4.1</em> Dímelo Instagram story on artisans


Tom Johnson, the head of audience development for the Guardian US, oversees the audience and social storytelling teams. He has spent the last decade working in a variety of audience strategy roles with an emphasis on data-driven experimentation, subscriber growth and creative visual storytelling.

"For publications of any size, it's important to think about what the primary audience objective is with any platform and then making sure that your work there ultimately supports that goal," said Johnson, who also has done audience engagement work with The Washington Post. "Social platforms can be useful in making strong first impressions with new readers, but it's important to consider the longer-term objective. If it's building an engaged community around a specific topic or coverage area, what are you doing to foster and encourage thoughtful discussion?"

The same is true for how to approach existing online communities as a publication. When Johnson was at the Post, he worked with the social media team to refine the Post's Reddit strategy and the Post's presence there across different subreddits.

"It's a strong example of how you can engage and grow an off-site audience over time by bringing added value to a discussion that falls within your coverage areas," he said. "This can take many forms -- hosting an AMA on an important news topic with your reporters who can provide expertise or simply finding a trending Reddit thread discussing one of your stories and commenting with additional context from your previous reporting."

Encouraging reader participation on a website is also an effective way to build an engaged audience over time. This can be done through recurring, consistent live chats or even incorporating reader input into live coverage, which is something that the Guardian's soccer live blog often does effectively.

"Creating regular programming with distinct voices, which the Guardian's live blogs are known for, can help build a connection with readers over time and hopefully turn some of those people who found the page via search into regular home page visitors," Johnson said.

:::: section
## How Big Is the Social Media Audience?

In its spring 2023 global social media research summary, Smart Insights reported that 60% of the world's population -- about 4.8 billion people -- uses social media. From 2022 to 2023, more than 150 million new social media users have come online, and the average daily usage is 2 hours and 24 minutes for all who are using the platforms.

The study also found that the rapid growth from TikTok showed no sign of slowing down: TikTok had 1.4 billion monthly active users in 2022 and was expected to reach 1.8 billion by the end of 2023.

Elon Musk's acquisition of Twitter has led to many unpopular changes to the platform, including paid verification checkmarks, metrics on viewership, an algorithmically curated "For You" feed, which mimics a popular feature on TikTok, and a "Following" tab. But for the first half of 2023, the platform has remained in principle a place to share news and interact with readers. Tools such as Post.news, Bluesky Social, Threads and Mastodon emerged as competitors, but many journalists continue posting to Twitter.

While some platforms continue to show growth for news consumption, social media news consumption overall has slowed worldwide, according to a 2023 Reuters Institute Digital News Report. The study said that news consumption on vertical video platforms like TikTok and Instagram has not grown fast enough to offset the reduction caused by Facebook's pullback of sharing news stories. A 2022 study by the Pew Research Center showed similar findings.

::: section
### ***Interactive Chart***

For years, news organizations have offered digital stories through e-newsletters, mobile apps, podcasts and other channels. All help direct more traffic to your website, but it's not enough.

That's where audience engagement and development come in, and social media plays a big role. Building social media campaigns around stories -- complete with teases, videos, graphics and other storytelling materials -- requires a strategic approach and requires people like Johnson to guide it.


<em>Figure</em> <em>4.2</em> Bar chart of active users on social platforms. Graphic/Mike Reilley.


At the Guardian US, Johnson finds that it helps to apply a scientific method to building audience campaigns -- start with a hypothesis, determine the best variables and data to track, run the experiment and then distill your findings into an analysis. His process is as follows:

1.  **Outline your audience goal at the beginning of the campaign -- and be specific.** The goals can be quantitative, qualitative or both, but they need to be established at the very beginning. For instance, let's say the goal is to grow the student media outlet's email newsletter list by 20% over the course of the semester. One of the first things they should do is conduct competitive research and see what other similarly sized student media outlets have tried in that space. What worked for them and what didn't work? Ideally, do some reporting and reach out to them.
2.  **Formulate a few hypotheses for what you will test.** Maybe it's a specific part of *how* you will accomplish this goal, or simply testing an assumption -- e.g., if we offer an inbox exclusive guest op-ed from a notable alumnus each week, this will drive newsletter signs up. Again, documentation is key. Make sure this lives in a document somewhere that you can revisit at the end of the campaign.
3.  **Track as much as data you can.** If you were to test the impact of visual Twitter threads on newsletter sign-ups, for instance, use a distinct [bit.ly](http://bit.ly) URL for those sign-up links so you can track exactly how many people are clicking on the link and then calculate a click-through rate based on the total number of tweet impressions. (More on analytics later in this chapter.)
4.  **At the end of the campaign, pull out your original doc and revisit those hypotheses.** What surprised you? Even if your hypotheses turn out to be wrong, there's still so much you can learn from the data. In fact, those moments when our initial hypotheses are wrong are often more useful in the long run and can inform your next experiment.
5.  **Document, document, document.** Don't wait until too long after the campaign to analyze the campaign and document your findings. What would you do differently next time? Which additional hypotheses do you wish you had included if you could do it over again? Chances are you'll have found the idea for your next campaign over the course of the experiment. If so, repeat the process. Over time, this will make future audience campaigns that much more effective, and you'll start to build out a library of useful insights.

In his book "JournalismNext," Mark Briggs writes that the challenge with social media campaigns is that not everyone is on all of the platforms and the audience keeps shifting. Pinterest and Tumblr were once all the rage, but not so much today. While TikTok has become a global phenomenon and produces results for some, others have left over privacy and security concerns. The same with Twitter. It's always a moving target.

Briggs offered some broad guidelines for targeting audiences on each platform:

- **Twitter:** Breaking news, sports, politics, business news, raw, real-time reactions to live news events and TV. It's also a great place to reach journalists, who hang out on Twitter a lot. I've used my \@journtoolbox Twitter account to target journalists with Journalist's Toolbox site updates.
- **Facebook:** The best referral source for website traffic, Briggs says. It continues to drive more clicks to news sites than Google, even after it shifted its algorithm in 2018.
- **Instagram:** This tends to attract lighter features, trend and pop culture stories from news outlets (see Dímelo's use of Instagram in the next section).
- **Pinterest:** Food, fashion, fitness and trends on this lifestyle network.
- **YouTube:** Beyond posting your content, this can be a reporting tool. Follow channels of the people and organizations that you cover. Create playlists of ongoing topics and issues in the community so people can follow them.
- **Reddit:** The community is very skeptical of news users, and can often reject posts that are relevant to various communities. Johnson said the Post built a presence on Reddit in part by engaging in subreddits where its journalism was already being shared and discussed. Sometimes this requires reaching out to the moderators, as each subreddit can have its own community rules.


<em>Figure</em> <em>4.3</em> Dímelo Snapchat on World Cup soccer


::: section
## Dímelo's Projects and Social Platforms

When Aguilar co-founded Dímelo, she and the students started by studying trends and habits on the various social platforms. Which ones would be the best fit for their stories?

Once Dímelo's research was complete, the platforms were built and the apps and tools were in place for the students, they had no problem finding stories to tell.

Here are a few that resonated with audiences across several social platforms. It's important to note that the concise writing and visual storytelling carry the stories, regardless of the social account it appears on.

**Snapchat**

- The staff covered the Rams in the Super Bowl, Día de los Muertos, street vendors, Latinas rising and more.

**Anxiety in the Latinx Community, a Spotify Playlist**

- This playlist combined original content with songs. It won several awards, including a national award in arts and entertainment in the podcasting/audio category.

**Instagram Stories**

- Dímelo produces several of these on a variety of topics, including a feature story on an artisan in Puerto Rico.

**Instagram Reels**

- Among their best projects is a tutorial on Día de los Muertos and its history.

**Instagram Video**

- Food stories generate a lot of traffic on social channels, including this Dímelo feature on hot sauce.

**TikTok: Kobe Bryant Memorial**

- TikTok can be more than just dance videos and silliness. Here, the student journalists cover the memorial to the former Los Angeles Lakers great gone far too soon.

Beyond Dímelo, Aguilar and her students also have experimented with print and online zines. Their LA Taco project proved to be an interesting case study. The students worked with the hyperlocal media outlet LA Taco to produce two zines. They are a membership-focused outlet, so they wanted to provide the zine as a perk to members and as a way to build membership.

"We distributed the bulk of zines to members and new members, and also targeted drops in the community, such as food trucks and community centers," Aguilar said. "LA Taco is a digital-only publication, so we felt that a print edition might increase visibility in certain communities."

\* \* \*


Aguilar trains her students to build impactful social media content. Her approach is:

- **Be genuine and personal.** Create content people can relate to.
- **Do your homework.** Social media content should be well researched and have strong reporting. Research trends and conversations. Look for unique angles. Include credible human voices and sources.
- **Design matters.** The look and feel of content is important. Developing a consistent brand and look can elevate your content. This takes time and planning. Some content can be developed on the fly and can break the mold, but if you have the time, plan it out.
- **Storyboard.** Plan it out if you have the time. Focus on hooks and what will grab attention in a compelling and powerful way.
- **Engage.** Reply to comments if possible and engage in conversations with your audience. Create callouts. Give your community the opportunity to participate in multiple ways. (See the next step too).
- **Try different formats.** Social isn't just one platform. Have community events IRL. Do a virtual event. Create a print zine. Create printed fliers. Think in multidimensional ways.
- **Be creative.** Experiment and try new things. Student media is an amazing sandbox where you may have almost limitless opportunities for creativity.
- **Change the norms.** Student media is one place to create new practices that move the industry forward, create space for all, embrace diversity in multiple forms and innovate.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Social Media: Past, Present and Future

Aguilar's approach to using targeted social media coverage has been honed over 18 years of working on social media. Since the emergence of Facebook (2004), Twitter (2006) and Instagram (2010), media outlets have scrambled to find ways to tell stories in a way that reached the Millennial and Gen Z audiences that flock to them for everything -- news, sports, entertainment and more.

The struggle early on was real. Beyond posting stories there, what else could journalists do with it?

Two early case studies were the "Miracle on the Hudson" in January 2009 and the summer 2009 Iran election protests and violence. When Sully Sullenberger famously landed US Airways Flight 1549 on the Hudson River, most of the early photos and video didn't come from news outlets. They came from eyewitnesses who were on ferry boats crossing the Hudson. Those images and videos were quickly shared over social media, and news outlets used them in their breaking coverage. Among the very first reports was a Twitter post by Janis Krums from Sarasota, Florida, who was on a ferry that arrived on the scene just a few minutes after the plane had crashed into the freezing water.

This is a technique called crowdsourcing -- where mainstream media ask for or search for eyewitness accounts from breaking stories they're not staffing or haven't arrived to yet. It's a best practice to ask the person posting who shot the photo and get that person's permission to use it, though you can embed tweets rights-free.

Several months after Sullenberger's water landing, violence broke out on the streets of Tehran, Iran, due to public unrest over the election results. State-sponsored media spread propaganda and any independent media were quickly shut down. But with social media, journalists, protesters, eyewitnesses -- everyone could post. There were no printing presses or TV satellites to shut down.

On June 15, The New York Times reported: "Iranians are blogging, posting to Facebook and, most visibly, coordinating their protests on Twitter. Their activity has increased, not decreased, since the presidential election on Friday and ensuing attempts by the government to restrict or censor their online communications."

While some of this was grounded in activism and rebellion by the protesters, personal accounts and reporting also became part of the discussion. Hashtags were shared. Video and photos of protesters being attacked emerged. Without social media channels, much of that would not have been seen by the millions who did access it.

Both of these landmark news events became case studies for how crowdsourcing and breaking news coverage would be altered for years to come.

\* \* \*

:::: section
## Crowdsourcing and Live-Tweeting a Murder Trial

Social media can be helpful for covering all kinds of breaking news events, including trials. Reporters can use Twitter, Facebook and other social platforms to background witnesses, find expert sources and the accused, provide updates during the trial and more.

In [Chapter 1](#ch01.xhtml__15031-5909_ch001), we learned how Pulitzer Prize winner Chris Graves reported on the high-profile Pike County murder trials. She used crowdsourcing and live-tweeting while covering the case as a Washington Post freelancer while also teaching a remote college course on the case for the University of Nebraska-Lincoln.

Sitting a few feet away from her in the courtroom was her longtime friend and former colleague Patti Gallagher Newberry, who was covering the case for the Cincinnati Enquirer and the USA Today Network.

Both reporters covered the trial using social media, but had different assignments.

Graves was writing more "big-picture" stories and backgrounding for a book she's writing on the murder trial of George Wagner IV, which began Aug. 29, 2022, and concluded Dec. 19 that year, when Wagner was sentenced to eight consecutive life sentences and 121 additional years for his role in the shooting deaths of seven members of southern Ohio's Rhoden family and one future member.

Newberry was writing wrap-up or look-ahead news features that focused on any legal moves or major developments that week.

Both reporters faced one major challenge: a ban on phones in the courtroom. This made it difficult for Newberry in particular, as she couldn't provide on-the-fly updates over Twitter without leaving the courtroom and potentially missing something important. So she and her editor agreed that she would tweet during breaks in the proceedings and post Twitter threads -- longer narratives where subtweets support a lead tweet -- at mid-morning, lunchtime, mid-afternoon and the end of the day.

"I would aim for a thread of five to 10 tweets from witnesses and other court action in the preceding hour or two," said Newberry, who added that she has a "general distaste for live-tweeting."

"I typically restricted threads to one topic, with a quote or two in each post. When possible, I grabbed photos from our photographer, who filed on a shared Google Drive for all participating media."

The approach worked. Newberry provided some masterful courtroom reporting over those updates and threads ([Figure 4.4](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_004)):

- Pike County Massacre: [https://twitter.com/pattinewberry/status/1598705789363003392](https://twitter.com)
- Key Testimony from the Trial: [https://twitter.com/pattinewberry/status/1597331981565538305](https://twitter.com)
- The Case Heads to Jury: [https://twitter.com/pattinewberry/status/1597720267580547074](https://twitter.com)
- Jury Deliberation: [https://twitter.com/pattinewberry/status/1597999709272297474](https://twitter.com)
- A Video Update from Outside the Courthouse: [https://twitter.com/pattinewberry/status/1597578868856070146?s=20&t=Jm5zw78z3nIvoBS6zog99g](https://twitter.com)


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_004"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.4</em></a> Newberry’s Twitter thread on jury deliberations


<em>Figure</em> <em>4.5</em> Newberry’s Twitter thread update on the Pike County murder trial


"I much preferred the threads to live tweets, because they allowed the introduction of more context and, to some extent, storytelling," she said. "I could then use those tweets -- which used every available character -- to populate daily stories or later wrap-up/look-ahead news features.

"I essentially made up this 'strategy' on my own, with limited prior Twitter use, as the best approach to our coverage. Many other journalists covering the story -- whether watching the live feed from the courthouse media room or watching remotely -- live-tweeted through the trial. I was committed to be in the courtroom as often as possible and convinced that episodic tweet threads had greater value than a stream of disconnected quotes and factoids."

Graves took a different approach. She often uses it to find expert sources and asks her followers what they're curious about and what questions they would ask. She DMs people to ask them questions and get in contact with them for potential interviews. She also sets up Twitter lists, a collection of specific accounts around a certain topic, which she did for the trial to follow key reporters who were covering the case. She also used the list to triple-check her story quotes and could see what other reporters were posting.

"The Civil-War era courtroom made it *very* difficult to hear during testimony at times," she said.

Other tools she used were:

- **Facebook:** She uses Facebook to follow specific audiences and to stay in contact with many of her sources. Sometimes, it is the best place to engage. In the Rhoden family case, she found many local sources this way who were afraid to meet her in person or discuss information. It is important, however, to set up your rules for engaging on these platforms: Are they on or off the record? Can you use information that people give you here? Can you use comments they make? Get the OK first.\
   Several Facebook groups were dedicated to the Rhoden case. Many of the people following it had contacts and insights into the case before the arrest, during the pending trials and even after the trials. Most of these groups are private, and Graves either asked or was asked to join.\
   She always made it clear that she would not offer her opinion about what she thought about the case, etc. But she would engage or ask questions if members had them. She found this to be beneficial during the reporting on this case because it has gone on for so long. Several members of these groups are very knowledgeable, and she would tap into that knowledge or query them on what they were most interested in and why.
- **Reddit:** In the early days of the investigation, she spent a lot of time on Reddit threads and local message boards scanning for information. The majority of the information was garbage, but it helped inform some of her reporting, such as discounting rumors, etc.

\* \* \*

<aside aria-label="box1" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## More Social Media Tips from Chris Graves for Covering Trials and Breaking News 

- Set up Twitter lists to cancel out all the Twitter noise.
- Research handles and hashtags so your content is distributed correctly.
- Do not speculate. Write only what you observe or know.
- Write conversationally. Strive to follow AP style, grammar and punctuation rules. Twitter is not kind to those who don't. But also know that you can use numerals and understood abbreviations to save room.
- Never delete tweets. But, do correct if you make an error. Most people forgive reporters who are live-tweeting. It is ruthless and hard -- especially if you are outside and it's cold, rainy or hot.
- Alert your audience when you will be live-tweeting. I try to include a photo of myself as I am beginning.
- Be serious when you need to, but also include your observations. LIke all good reporting, Twitter is a great place to try to put your reader into a scene. People want you to take them there as much as possible.
- If you are in court, follow the rules of the court. In the Rhoden trial and in federal court: No laptops or phones were allowed during 99% of the trial, which meant live-tweeting happened between breaks as a summary of what had just occurred. Other days, if testimony was being streamed, I watched in my car or the media room and tweeted as I watched. I did not do this very often, however, as part of my work needed to happen in the courtroom.
  
  
  <em>Figure</em> <em>4.6</em> Photos Graves shot from protests when she was working for Minnesota Public Radio.
  
- I am not great at video, so I don't often record myself on Twitter. But I enjoy when other reporters do either before or after an event. I do use video, however, to share scenes (i.e.: Trump rally/protests).
- Live-tweeting is the best on-deadline writing there is: There is often little time to overwrite or overthink. We write the newest news/the most interesting/most compelling -- which is *exactly* what makes for the most powerful inverted pyramid/summary leads and stories.
- It's time-consuming, but when I write Twitter threads. I write live on Twitter and thread as I go, but I do think there is something to be said for writing in a file and then moving that over to Twitter in storyform. Number your threaded tweets to make it easier for your reader. Threaded tweets can be unfurled as well and often are an excellent way to create a running notebook of your coverage.

</aside>

\* \* \*

Examples of Graves' Twitter threads from the case:

- **Closing arguments:** [https://twitter.com/chrisgraves/status/1597328363307544576](https://twitter.com)
- **Cross-examination:** [https://twitter.com/chrisgraves/status/1593363811284619266](https://twitter.com)
- **Writing with context during testimony:** [https://twitter.com/chrisgraves/status/1593363798131232768](https://twitter.com)
- Graves: "I love the writing in this one and shows that you shouldn't be afraid that if you know your material, to write with some bit of authority -- I have covered this case for \[many\] years at the time of the trial and I can write about the twists and turns because I have first-hand knowledge and experience. Do this only if you have the knowledge, facts and experience."


<em>Figure</em> <em>4.7</em> Chris Graves’ Twitter thread from closing arguments of the case

::: section
## ChatGPT for Writing Twitter Threads

[Chapter 11](#ch11.xhtml__15031-5909_ch011) features an exercise using ChatGPT plug-in ScholarAI to pull research. It also covers how you can turn that research into a Twitter thread by using this prompt:

> *Now turn the research into a Twitter thread with five viral hooks and hashtags*

In my experiments with the prompt it's produced some good threads that cite sources and need only minor tweaks to meet publishable standards. It's worth a try, especially if you're stuck.

You also can write a thread after pasting an article into ChatGPT (or Google Bard) and asking it to write a thread based on it. It works well for summaries, too.

For more ChatGPT plug-ins, visit JournalistsToolbox.ai.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Downloading Video from Twitter

If you need to download a video or a GIF from a tweet, just reply to the tweet and mention \@getvideobot. The bot will retrieve the video in seconds and leave a link to download it in the mentions. Warning: Video and GIFs can be subject to copyright. Make sure you get permission from the owner/author to use them first.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Writing Twitter Threads

Twitter threads aren't just for breaking news stories. They're also an effective way to roll out a big investigative project on social media. This works particularly well with data-driven projects that feature many visualizations that can be attached to the posts. A popular approach is to keep a "data diary" of the reporting, analysis and writing/editing process, then explain that reporting process to the reader through the Twitter thread. This makes your reporting transparent and builds trust with the reader. Here are some examples:

- **ProPublica Illinois: UIC and FOIA Law**
- [https://twitter.com/jodiscohen/status/1108417904830238721?s=12](https://twitter.com)
- This thread is an excellent behind-the-scenes look at the public records reporting that goes into an investigative story.
- **ProPublica: Migrant Children**
- [https://twitter.com/ProPublica/status/1020365023171014664](https://twitter.com)
- This thread focused on an investigative piece on migrant children being separated from their parents after ICE began its detention areas in Texas in June. It shows depth, and note how they broke down each tweet by person/issue. It really "put a face" on the story, rather than just sharing a bunch of statistics.
- **BBC Africa: Anatomy of a Killing**
- [https://twitter.com/BBCAfrica/status/1044186344153583616](https://twitter.com)
- This thread combines fact-checking techniques with visualizations using Google Earth tools to geolocate and verify the location of a gruesome murder of women and children in Cameroon. The BBC then used the Twitter thread to explain the process of how they proved the Cameroon government was lying to cover up the military's involvement. [Chapter 8](#ch08.xhtml__15031-5909_ch008) examines this case study in greater detail with Google Earth.
- **Sarah Smith: Fact-Checking Ben Carson on Houston Housing**
- [https://twitter.com/sarahesmith23/status/1130975400732106758?s=12](https://twitter.com)
- The Houston Chronicle's housing reporter used data and fact-checking skills to correct U.S. Secretary of Housing and Urban Development Ben Carson in this Twitter thread.
- **Student Projects**
- In the early days of the COVID-19 pandemic, student reporters for The Red Line Project at the University of Illinois Chicago built several visualizations from global, national and local datasets. They kept data diaries as they downloaded, cleaned and analyzed the data. They logged background on how they visualized the data and how it fit contextually into the story.

UIC senior Chris Katsaros and his team used a Twitter thread to explain reporting on Illinois COVID-19 cases. Senior Charles Tharpe used a Twitter thread to highlight key points in his team's story on COVID-19 and the increase in domestic violence during the pandemic's first few months.

Incorporating graphics, video and photos into the Twitter threads helps readers notice the threads in their feeds as well as convey good data and background for the story. It's a smart way to leverage all of your digital assets for both the story and the social posts.

\* \* \*

::: section
## Pro Tip: Social Media Policies

When you are hired at a news outlet, ask to see that newsroom's social media policy and ethics code the day you start. Many newsrooms have you read them and sign an agreement that you follow it. It's important to be clear on the organization's rules and expectations. It can greatly vary by medium and chain/outlet.

Some job candidates even ask to see the policies during the interview process so they're clear on where the company stands on ethics or if social media guidelines are too stringent. Be sure to protect yourself.

\* \* \*

::::::: section
## Social Media Safety: Dealing with Trolls and More

Gamergate and the misogynist online attacks against women have been commonplace since social media first emerged. Female sports journalists, in particular, are targets of aggressive attacks and even threats of physical harm.

In 2014, Michelle Ferrier founded Troll-Busters ([www.troll-busters.com](http://www.troll-busters.com)), a resource for journalists and others dealing with online harassment to seek help. A former journalist and columnist, Ferrier received hate mail and death threats for writing a lifestyle column in her local paper. Troll-Busters was her method of saying, "Enough!"

The site offers training, research, tips and contacts for getting help when harassment occurs. The Committee to Protect Journalists and the Student Press Law Center also offer resources.

There are many types of harassment ([Figure 4.8](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_008)): implied and explicit threats, doxing, libel, impersonating your account and posting sexually explicit material, among them. The response depends on the severity and how often the threats occur, but there are some basic steps you can take to help deal with the threats:

- **You are not alone:** Seek help from your editors and colleagues. Make sure you report it to your employer right away and ask for help. Ask a colleague to monitor your social media accounts and take a break from it. Talk to colleagues who have been through this; they're always eager to offer good advice in these situations. Also ask for help from organizations like Troll-Busters and the Student Press Law Center.
- **Block and report:** Block the account harassing you and report them to the app or platform. Ask colleagues to do the same and get the account removed. Muting the offensive account temporarily is an option, but not if the person is making threats.
- **Take care of yourself:** Dealing with trolls and online harassment can take a toll on your mental health.

Some other resources that can help:

- TRFilter
- [www.trfilter.org/](http://www.trfilter.org)
- This tool from Thomson Reuters allows users to document and manage online harassment and abuse, so they can take control of their social media feeds and focus on their professional activities.
- Dart Center: Self-Care Tips for Journalists
- Troll-Busters also encourages taking pre-emptive measures to ward off harassment before it occurs. In [Figure 4.9](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_009), Troll-Busters outlines some basic and even extreme measures some journalists need to take.

\* \* \*


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_008"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.8</em></a> Trollbusters graphic: Where Threats Occur


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_009"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.9</em></a> Journalists should set boundaries to help protect themselves against harassment


<aside aria-label="box3" class="box1" epub:type="tip" role="doc-tip" title="box">

::: section
## Privacy Tools 

Staying safe online often means keeping your confidential sources protected from hackers and eavesdropping third parties as well. There are many good encrypted messaging apps available, but here are three of the best:

::: section
## Signal 

State-of-the-art end-to-end encryption keeps your conversations secure. This tool is very popular among investigative reporters who want to keep conversations with confidential sources secret.

::: section
## Telegram 

Another encrypted messaging tool. What makes Telegram unique is its focus on privacy and an open-source API. It also allows multiple devices to use the same account and multiple accounts on the same device.

::: section
## WhatsApp 

More than 2 billion people in over 180 countries use this free app. With end-to-end encryption, your personal messages and calls are secured. Only you and the person you're talking to can read or listen to them, and nobody in between.

Journalists also can turn off tracking on their phones and adjust Google Maps settings so it won't track and store past locations. This is especially important when traveling abroad or covering areas of conflict.

More privacy tools and apps are in [Chapter 11](#ch11.xhtml__15031-5909_ch011)'s digital security section

</aside>

\* \* \*

::: section
## Fact-Checking on Social Media

If you are passing through the American History Museum at the Smithsonian in Washington, D.C., you may see an early version of an iPhone on display. The phone once belonged to former NPR journalist Andy Carvin, who used his Twitter app on that phone to cover the Arab Spring in 2011.

Carwin communicated with protesters in the Middle East over Twitter to verify eyewitness accounts from the front lines. He debunked rumors, crowdsourced, fact-checked photos and built an elaborate network of sources and fellow fact-checkers. Carvin's widely considered the first journalist to use Twitter in such a way.

In a world where everything is true and nothing is true on the Internet, fact-checking over social channels can be challenging. Misinformation and disinformation flowed freely during the early years of the COVID-19 outbreak. Former President Donald Trump's Twitter account was a conga line of disinformation and attacks on the press.

There are some basic steps we can take to verify what we find online. Here's a list of steps to follow, taken from some of my own lectures as well as the book "Social and Mobile Media Journalism" (Routledge, Adornato):

1.  **Be skeptical.** If it's too good to be true, it probably is. If there's a link to a story or a graphic, click on it. Always read past the headline and check sourcing and attribution in the story.
2.  **Review the original source's social media accounts.** What else is the person or organization posting? Is it reliable? Who are they retweeting or sharing content from? How long has the account been around?
3.  **Cross-check the information with other sources.** Contact the source directly. Ask for a phone number. Vet them. It's worth the extra few minutes to get it right.
4.  **Check the geo-location of the post.** Where is the post coming from?
5.  **Seek corroboration on social media.** If someone sends you a photo of a fire in a downtown Chicago high-rise building, do an image search on it to see if it appears elsewhere online. Search for other photos and eyewitness accounts on social channels.
6.  **Seek official corroboration.** Call the fire department to confirm the fire. Look it up online.
7.  **If an image seems suspicious**, **reverse image search it in Google ([images.google.com](https://www.images.google.com)).** Check images' metadata if you can. Many social channels remove metadata and Exif data from the image when you post. But Twitter and some other tools now give users the chance to include Alt information by tapping on the photo. Any image you see with "Alt" in the lower left corner includes information about the photo. Tap on it to see.
8.  **Use Google Earth to verify photo locations.** More on this in [Chapter 8](#ch08.xhtml__15031-5909_ch008).

::: section
## Social Media Analytics

Earlier in this chapter, Aguilar and Johnson talked about how to engage audiences over social media. But to do that, you need analytics to measure success. What's the best time of day to tweet? Which beats or topics are generating the most Facebook shares? Which are getting the most engagement and replies/comments from the audience? What stories get the least engagement?

These are all questions reporters and editors need to track on a daily basis. Many newsrooms have large monitors running Chartbeat, Sprout Social, Buffer or other analytics tools to measure website and or social media traffic. Google Analytics will tell you when and what social platforms are generating traffic to the site.

\* \* \*


::: section
## Measure Twitter Performance

One of the best ways to experiment with social media analytics -- for free -- is to log in to your Twitter account and go to [https://analytics.twitter.com/](https://analytics.twitter.com) ([Figure 4.10](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_010)).

This interface gives you an overview of your top-performing tweets, impressions (people who have scrolled by your post), profile views, follower count, mentions and more.

Now click on the "Tweets" tab in the top left corner of the page and you'll get a more detailed interface that breaks down the top-performing tweets. Scroll through and look at what topics have done well to get a quick snapshot of performances ([Figure 4.11](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_011)).


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_010"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.10</em></a> @journtoolbox Twitter analytics home page on May 24, 2023


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_011"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.11</em></a> @jourtoolbox Twitter activity and analytics


Here you can see your top performing tweets, replies, etc. Again, it's a good, quick snapshot to see how your account is performing.

In the upper right corner of the interface, you can select various dates from a pulldown menu. It defaults to the last 28 days, but you can shorten or lengthen that time in the pulldown. Once you have dates set, click on the "export data" button to the right, select "By Tweet" and a spreadsheet of all of your tweets over that timespan will download.

Open the spreadsheet in Google Sheets and you can sort the data by various analytics labeled at the top of each column ([Figure 4.12](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_012)): impressions, retweets, engagements, replies, URL clicks, etc. Sort them from highest to lowest and widen column C so you can see the content of the tweet. Look for these trends over time:

- Which tweets perform well, and which ones do not?
- Is there a certain day of the week that tweets perform better? A specific time?
- What topics/trends are doing well (sports, entertainment, types of news stories, etc.)?

Apply this analysis to any of your social media analytics to find these trends. It's important to measure them over several weeks and months before making any brash decisions about adjusting coverage and resources. I always use analytics to look at what we can change or improve in coverage, design or presentation rather than what can be cut.

**[**https://bit.ly/journaliststoolbox**](https://bit.ly/journaliststoolbox)**


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_012"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.12</em></a> Google Sheet with @journtoolbox analytics (Link to the spreadsheet: <a href="https://bit.ly/journaliststoolbox">https://bit.ly/journaliststoolbox</a>)


:::: section
## Putting Analytics to Work

One of the first things I did as the founding digital production director of Cronkite News at Arizona State's Walter Cronkite School of Journalism in 2014 was teach my students in the bureau how to look at website and social media traffic. We used Chartbeat, Google Analytics, YouTube, Twitter and other social analytics tools to write weekly traffic reports that were circulated among the newsroom at Cronkite News, which airs weeknights on Arizona PBS.

The reports included charts and graphics showing traffic trends, top-performing stories, tweets, etc. The Cronkite News broadcast team used those analytic reports to design a Friday newscast called Cronkite News Refresh that ran for several years. The 30-minute show featured the top-performing stories, tweets and Facebook posts/comments of the week. Producers embedded the social posts as pop-ups into the stories as they aired. It became a popular way to wrap up the week's newscasts -- all driven by website and social media analytics.

\* \* \*

::: section

## Visualizations on Social Media

Journalists often use text cards -- graphics that focus on a single stat or quote -- to feature on Twitter posts teasing a story. These text cards can be easily built with tools such as Canva (desktop and app), Adobe Post or Photoshop (desktop and app) and other tools that are detailed in [Chapter 6](#ch06.xhtml__15031-5909_ch006).

They also can be built as animated GIFs with Google Data GIF Maker and other tools. These tools are handy for breaking stories -- election night analysis, highlighting a stat or quote from a study or speech, budget figures, crime statistics and more.

Some journalists pull graphics from their stories and attach them to tweets and provide a link to the story. The graphics grab readers' attention as they scroll through Facebook or Twitter. Canva can be used to make text cards for Instagram stories. Apps such as Chartistic and Icongraph help journalists build charts on a phone or tablet that can easily be shared to social channels.

National Public Radio's Social Media Desk created an excellent style guide detailing the types of social graphics you can build for stories. It provides links to tools, examples and sizing recommendations. While dated, it's still an excellent resource for thinking of social media graphic ideas.

[Vidyo.ai](http://Vidyo.ai) is an AI-driven video editing tool that can trim a long video into several short social media clips in about a minute. The tool makes logical cuts that work as audiograms and other clips. You just load the three-minute or longer video (or paste a link to it) in the browser-based tool and in a matter of minutes, you have your clips.

It lets you select which social channel you want to create videos for: TikTok, Instagram, Facebook, etc. After about a minute, the tool will edit over 30 short clips from the video that will work in the platforms you selected. It emails you a reminder when the clips are done processing.

I tested it on video of Chicago Mayor Brandon Johnson's election-night victory speech and it produced some top-notch edits and clips ([Figure 4.13](#ch04.xhtml__15031-5909_ch004_Figure_013)). But it did struggle in spots with transcription. For instance, the words "to choose" in the speech were transcribed as "tool."

The free verison of the tool gives you up to 75 minutes of video edits per month. Paid packages offer more minutes and additional features, such as adding logos and branding. Prices range from \$300 to \$500 per year.


<a href="#ch04.xhtml_R_15031-5909_ch004_Figure_013"><em>Figure</em> <em>4.13</em></a> <a href="http://Vidyo.io">Vidyo.io</a> video editing interface with clips from Chicago Mayor Brandon Johnson’s victory speech

## Key Social Media Tools

Beyond Twitter, Facebook, TikTok and the other obvious apps, here are some complementary social media tools that can help with download, analysis, workflow and more.

- Hootsuite
- <https://hootsuite.com>
- Manage multiple social accounts on one dashboard. Compress links and schedule posts.
- TweetGPT
- A chrome extension that uses ChatGPT to write tweets.
- Twitonomy
- Tool can be used to observe a specific account's activity and quickly identify signs of suspicious behavior. You also can browse, sort and remove people from your lists.
- [TWDown.net](http://TWDown.net): Twitter Video Downloader
- Postwise.ai
- AI-driven Twitter writing tool
- Keyhole.co
- Track hashtags in real time.
- Social Searcher
- Collates postings on social media networks.
- LinkedIn for Journalists
- Does training tailored to journos every few weeks.

## What's in Dímelo's Toolbox? 

Students experiment with a variety of mobile apps and desktop tools when building stories for Dímelo social media channels. But Aguilar targeted five critical ones every social media creator should have.

"Canva is probably the most popular tool we use for social media, almost on a daily basis," Aguilar said. "Adobe Rush is great for mobile video as well as Apple Caps. Anchor is amazing for quick audio edits and podcasts. I know people have said GIFs are dead, but we use Giphy all the time for the memes."

- Allows researchers to collect a dataset of TikToks associated with specific hashtags over lengthier periods.

- Live map of public stories people are sharing on Snapchat.

- A non-public Facebook keyword search for people who work in the public interest. It allows you to search keywords on specific dates. You are granted access because of your work.

- Scan Facebook profiles to see where people are posting.

- Some great tips in this Medium post by Tshepo Tshabalala.
- TweetGram
- Makes it possible for users to automatically tweet their Instagram photos and videos on Twitter. This will help to save time because they only have to post once on Instagram and have it on Twitter automatically.
- Gramfeed
- An Instagram search tool.

## Exercises

::: section
### ***Exercise 1: Live-Tweeting***

Based on what you learned in this chapter, find a public meeting or speech to cover on or off campus. Live-tweet from it. Begin with an introduction and a photo from where you are, then tweet what is said or what happens. If the city council takes a vote on something, tweet it. If someone says something, share it. Share photos and describe what is happening around you. Shoot for 20--25 tweets maximum. Sports events work well for this assignment, too.

::: section
### ***Exercise 2: Social Media Graphic***

Pull data from the Brewers Association on craft brewery sales by state (start with yours). Pull a few statistics and put them into a graphics program such as Jitter, Canva or Infogram. Then export the static graphic, attach it to a tweet and share. The graphic also can work in a story, should you write one.

- Brewers Association data: [www.brewersassociation.org/statistics-and-data/state-craft-beer-stats](http://www.brewersassociation.org)

::: section
### ***Exercise 3: AI Split-Testing on Social Media Posts***

Have ChatGPT or Google Bard (see [Chapter 9](#ch09.xhtml__15031-5909_ch009) for details) write some Twitter or other social media posts for three stories. Then have one of your social media editors write posts for the same stories. Share both and see which get better engagement: the person or the machine.

::: section
### ***Exercise 4: Develop a Targeted Social Media Channel***

Based on what you learned about Dímelo in this chapter, form teams of three or four and research a specific audience or topic you want to reach on social media. What's the best channel to use? The audience, and what content are they looking for? How can you shape the channel's voice to reach that audience?

Do your research, and present concepts to the class or newsroom later in the year.
